     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_power" title="Solar power">
       Solar power
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      <b>
       Solar energy
      </b>
      is radiant
      <a href="/wiki/Light" title="Light">
       light
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Heat" title="Heat">
       heat
      </a>
      from the
      <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">
       Sun
      </a>
      that is harnessed using a range of ever-evolving technologies such as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_heating" title="Solar heating">
       solar heating
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaics" title="Photovoltaics">
       photovoltaics
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_thermal_energy" title="Solar thermal energy">
       solar thermal energy
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_architecture" title="Solar architecture">
       solar architecture
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Artificial_photosynthesis" title="Artificial photosynthesis">
       artificial photosynthesis
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ie11_1-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ie11-1">
        [1]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-2">
        [2]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      It is an important source of
      <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">
       renewable energy
      </a>
      and its technologies are broadly characterized as either
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Passive_solar" title="Passive solar">
       passive solar
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Active_solar" title="Active solar">
       active solar
      </a>
      depending on how they capture and distribute solar energy or convert it into
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_power" title="Solar power">
       solar power
      </a>
      . Active solar techniques include the use of
      <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_system" title="Photovoltaic system">
       photovoltaic systems
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Concentrated_solar_power" title="Concentrated solar power">
       concentrated solar power
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_water_heating" title="Solar water heating">
       solar water heating
      </a>
      to harness the energy. Passive solar techniques include orienting a building to the Sun, selecting materials with favorable
      <a href="/wiki/Thermal_mass" title="Thermal mass">
       thermal mass
      </a>
      or light-dispersing properties, and designing spaces that
      <a href="/wiki/Ventilation_(architecture)" title="Ventilation (architecture)">
       naturally circulate air
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      The large magnitude of solar energy available makes it a highly appealing source of electricity. The
      <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Development_Programme" title="United Nations Development Programme">
       United Nations Development Programme
      </a>
      in its 2000 World Energy Assessment found that the annual potential of solar energy was 1,575–49,837
      <a href="/wiki/Joule#Multiples" title="Joule">
       exajoules
      </a>
      (EJ). This is several times larger than the total
      <a href="/wiki/World_energy_consumption" title="World energy consumption">
       world energy consumption
      </a>
      , which was 559.8 EJ in 2012.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-3">
        [3]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World_Energy_Assessment_4-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-World_Energy_Assessment-4">
        [4]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 2011, the
      <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">
       International Energy Agency
      </a>
      said that "the development of affordable, inexhaustible and clean solar energy technologies will have huge longer-term benefits. It will increase countries’ energy security through reliance on an indigenous, inexhaustible and mostly import-independent resource, enhance
      <a href="/wiki/Sustainability" title="Sustainability">
       sustainability
      </a>
      , reduce pollution, lower the costs of mitigating
      <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">
       global warming
      </a>
      , and keep
      <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">
       fossil fuel
      </a>
      prices lower than otherwise. These advantages are global. Hence the additional costs of the incentives for early deployment should be considered learning investments; they must be wisely spent and need to be widely shared".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ie11_1-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-ie11-1">
        [1]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

     <p>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Potential">
       Potential
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Further information:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_radiation" title="Solar radiation">
       Solar radiation
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tmulti tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:229px;max-width:229px">
       <div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:227px;max-width:227px">
        <div class="thumbimage">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Breakdown_of_the_incoming_solar_energy.svg">
          <img alt="" data-file-height="580" data-file-width="800" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Breakdown_of_the_incoming_solar_energy.svg/225px-Breakdown_of_the_incoming_solar_energy.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Breakdown_of_the_incoming_solar_energy.svg/338px-Breakdown_of_the_incoming_solar_energy.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Breakdown_of_the_incoming_solar_energy.svg/450px-Breakdown_of_the_incoming_solar_energy.svg.png 2x" width="225"/>
         </a>
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
         About half the incoming solar energy reaches the Earth's surface.
        </div>
       </div>
       <div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:227px;max-width:227px">
        <div class="thumbimage">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Solar_land_area.png">
          <img alt="" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="680" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Solar_land_area.png/225px-Solar_land_area.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Solar_land_area.png/338px-Solar_land_area.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Solar_land_area.png/450px-Solar_land_area.png 2x" width="225"/>
         </a>
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
         Average
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Insolation" title="Insolation">
          insolation
         </a>
         . The theoretical area of the small black dots is sufficient to supply the
         <a href="/wiki/World_energy_consumption" title="World energy consumption">
          world's total energy needs
         </a>
         of 18
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terawatt" title="Terawatt">
          TW
         </a>
         with solar power.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The Earth receives 174,000
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terawatt" title="Terawatt">
       terawatts
      </a>
      (TW) of incoming solar radiation (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Insolation" title="Insolation">
       insolation
      </a>
      ) at the upper
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Earth's atmosphere">
       atmosphere
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-5">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Approximately 30% is reflected back to space while the rest is absorbed by clouds, oceans and land masses. The
      <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_spectrum" title="Electromagnetic spectrum">
       spectrum
      </a>
      of solar light at the Earth's surface is mostly spread across the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visible_light" title="Visible light">
       visible
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Near-infrared" title="Near-infrared">
       near-infrared
      </a>
      ranges with a small part in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Near-ultraviolet" title="Near-ultraviolet">
       near-ultraviolet
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-6">
        [6]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Most of the world's population live in areas with insolation levels of 150-300 watts/m², or 3.5-7.0
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilowatt-hour" title="Kilowatt-hour">
       kWh
      </a>
      /m² per day.
     </p>
     <p>
      Solar radiation is absorbed by the Earth's land surface,
      <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">
       oceans
      </a>
      – which cover about 71% of the globe – and atmosphere. Warm air containing evaporated water from the oceans rises, causing
      <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_circulation" title="Atmospheric circulation">
       atmospheric circulation
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Convection" title="Convection">
       convection
      </a>
      . When the air reaches a high altitude, where the temperature is low, water vapor condenses into clouds, which rain onto the Earth's surface, completing the
      <a href="/wiki/Water_cycle" title="Water cycle">
       water cycle
      </a>
      . The
      <a href="/wiki/Latent_heat" title="Latent heat">
       latent heat
      </a>
      of water condensation amplifies convection, producing atmospheric phenomena such as wind,
      <a href="/wiki/Cyclone" title="Cyclone">
       cyclones
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-cyclone" title="Anti-cyclone">
       anti-cyclones
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-7">
        [7]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Sunlight absorbed by the oceans and land masses keeps the surface at an average temperature of 14 °C.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-8">
        [8]
       </a>
      </sup>
      By
      <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">
       photosynthesis
      </a>
      , green plants convert solar energy into chemically stored energy, which produces food, wood and the
      <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">
       biomass
      </a>
      from which fossil fuels are derived.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-9">
        [9]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The total solar energy absorbed by Earth's atmosphere, oceans and land masses is approximately 3,850,000
      <a href="/wiki/Joule#Multiples" title="Joule">
       exajoules
      </a>
      (EJ) per year.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smil_2006.2C_p._12_10-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Smil_2006.2C_p._12-10">
        [10]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 2002, this was more energy in one hour than the world used in one year.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-11">
        [11]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-12">
        [12]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Photosynthesis captures approximately 3,000 EJ per year in biomass.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
       <a href="#cite_note-13">
        [13]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The amount of solar energy reaching the surface of the planet is so vast that in one year it is about twice as much as will ever be obtained from all of the Earth's non-renewable resources of coal, oil, natural gas, and mined uranium combined,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-14">
        [14]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <table class="wikitable floatleft" style="margin-right: 12px;">
      <tr>
       <th colspan="3" style="font-weight: normal;">
        <b>
         Yearly solar fluxes &amp; human consumption
        </b>
        <sup>
         1
        </sup>
       </th>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Solar
       </td>
       <td align="right">
        3,850,000
       </td>
       <td align="center">
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smil_2006.2C_p._12_10-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-Smil_2006.2C_p._12-10">
          [10]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Wind
       </td>
       <td align="right">
        2,250
       </td>
       <td align="center">
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
         <a href="#cite_note-15">
          [15]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Biomass potential
       </td>
       <td align="right">
        ~200
       </td>
       <td align="center">
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
         <a href="#cite_note-16">
          [16]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        <a href="/wiki/World_energy_consumption" title="World energy consumption">
         Primary energy use
        </a>
        <sup>
         2
        </sup>
       </td>
       <td align="right">

       </td>
       <td align="center">
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">

         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        Electricity
        <sup>
         2
        </sup>
       </td>
       <td align="right">
        ~67
       </td>
       <td align="center">
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
         <a href="#cite_note-18">

         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <th colspan="3" style="font-size: 80%; font-weight: normal; text-align: left; padding: 6px 2px 4px 4px;">
        <sup>
         1
        </sup>
        Energy given in
        <a href="/wiki/Orders_of_magnitude_(energy)" title="Orders of magnitude (energy)">
         Exajoule
        </a>
        (EJ) = 10
        <sup>
         18
        </sup>
        <a href="/wiki/Joule" title="Joule">
         J
        </a>
        = 278
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terawatt-hour" title="Terawatt-hour">
         TWh
        </a>
        <br/>
        <sup>
         2
        </sup>
        Consumption as of year 2010
       </th>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      The potential solar energy that could be used by humans differs from the amount of solar energy present near the surface of the planet because factors such as geography, time variation, cloud cover, and the land available to humans limit the amount of solar energy that we can acquire.
     </p>
     <p>
      Geography affects solar energy potential because areas that are closer to the
      <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">
       equator
      </a>
      have a greater amount of solar radiation. However, the use of
      <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaics" title="Photovoltaics">
       photovoltaics
      </a>
      that can follow the position of the sun can significantly increase the solar energy potential in areas that are farther from the equator.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World_Energy_Assessment_4-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-World_Energy_Assessment-4">
        [4]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Time variation effects the potential of solar energy because during the nighttime there is little solar radiation on the surface of the Earth for
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_panels" title="Solar panels">
       solar panels
      </a>
      to absorb. This limits the amount of energy that solar panels can absorb in one day.
      <a href="/wiki/Cloud_cover" title="Cloud cover">
       Cloud cover
      </a>
      can affect the potential of solar panels because clouds block incoming light from the sun and reduce the light available for solar cells.
     </p>
     <p>
      In addition, land availability has a large effect on the available solar energy because solar panels can only be set up on land that is otherwise unused and suitable for solar panels. Roofs have been found to be a suitable place for solar cells, as many people have discovered that they can collect energy directly from their homes this way. Other areas that are suitable for solar cells are lands that are not being used for businesses where solar plants can be established.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World_Energy_Assessment_4-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-World_Energy_Assessment-4">
        [4]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Solar technologies are characterized as either passive or active depending on the way they capture, convert and distribute sunlight and enable solar energy to be harnessed at different levels around the world, mostly depending on distance from the equator. Although solar energy refers primarily to the use of solar radiation for practical ends, all renewable energies, other than
      <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_power" title="Geothermal power">
       Geothermal power
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Tidal_power" title="Tidal power">
       Tidal power
      </a>
      , derive their energy either directly or indirectly from the Sun.
     </p>
     <p>
      Active solar techniques use photovoltaics,
      <a href="/wiki/Concentrated_solar_power" title="Concentrated solar power">
       concentrated solar power
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_thermal_collector" title="Solar thermal collector">
       solar thermal collectors
      </a>
      , pumps, and fans to convert sunlight into useful outputs. Passive solar techniques include selecting materials with favorable thermal properties, designing spaces that naturally circulate air, and referencing the position of a building to the Sun. Active solar technologies increase the supply of energy and are considered
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supply_side" title="Supply side">
       supply side
      </a>
      technologies, while passive solar technologies reduce the need for alternate resources and are generally considered demand side technologies.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEA_Solar_Thermal_19-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-IEA_Solar_Thermal-19">
        [19]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 2000, the
      <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Development_Programme" title="United Nations Development Programme">
       United Nations Development Programme
      </a>
      , UN Department of Economic and Social Affairs, and
      <a href="/wiki/World_Energy_Council" title="World Energy Council">
       World Energy Council
      </a>
      published an estimate of the potential solar energy that could be used by humans each year that took into account factors such as insolation, cloud cover, and the land that is usable by humans. The estimate found that solar energy has a global potential of 1,575–49,837 EJ per year
      <i>
       (see table below)
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World_Energy_Assessment_4-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-World_Energy_Assessment-4">
        [4]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <table class="wikitable" style="text-align: center; font-size: 0.9em;">
      <caption>
       Annual solar energy potential by region (Exajoules)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World_Energy_Assessment_4-4">

        </a>
       </sup>
      </caption>
      <tr>

      </tr>

        <i>

        </i>

          Total global annual solar energy potential amounts to 1,575 EJ (minimum) to 49,837 EJ (maximum)
         </li>
         <li>
          Data reflects assumptions of annual clear sky irradiance, annual average sky clearance, and available land area. All figures given in Exajoules.
         </li>
        </ul>
        <p>
         <i>
          Quantitative relation
         </i>
         of global solar potential vs. the world's
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Primary_energy_consumption" title="Primary energy consumption">
          primary energy consumption
         </a>
         :
        </p>
        <ul>
         <li>
          Ratio of potential vs. current consumption (402 EJ) as of year: 3.9 (minimum) to 124 (maximum)
         </li>
         <li>
          Ratio of potential vs. projected consumption by 2050 (590–1,050 EJ): 1.5–2.7 (minimum) to 47–84 (maximum)
         </li>
         <li>
          Ratio of potential vs. projected consumption by 2100 (880–1,900 EJ): 0.8–1.8 (minimum) to 26–57 (maximum)
         </li>
        </ul>
        <p>
         <i>
          Source:
         </i>
         <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Development_Programme" title="United Nations Development Programme">
          United Nations Development Programme
         </a>
         – World Energy Assessment (2000)
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World_Energy_Assessment_4-5">
          <a href="#cite_note-World_Energy_Assessment-4">
           [4]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </p>
       </th>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Thermal_energy">
       Thermal energy
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_thermal_energy" title="Solar thermal energy">
       Solar thermal energy
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Solar thermal technologies can be used for water heating, space heating, space cooling and process heat generation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
       <a href="#cite_note-20">
        [20]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Early_commercial_adaptation">
       Early commercial adaptation
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:US_Patent_1240890.pdf">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1650" data-file-width="1275" height="233" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/US_Patent_1240890.pdf/page1-180px-US_Patent_1240890.pdf.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/US_Patent_1240890.pdf/page1-270px-US_Patent_1240890.pdf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/US_Patent_1240890.pdf/page1-360px-US_Patent_1240890.pdf.jpg 2x" width="180"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:US_Patent_1240890.pdf" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        1917 Patent drawing of Shuman's solar collector
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In 1897,
      <a href="/wiki/Frank_Shuman" title="Frank Shuman">
       Frank Shuman
      </a>
      , a U.S. inventor, engineer and solar energy pioneer built a small demonstration solar engine that worked by reflecting solar energy onto square boxes filled with ether, which has a lower boiling point than water, and were fitted internally with black pipes which in turn powered a steam engine. In 1908 Shuman formed the Sun Power Company with the intent of building larger solar power plants. He, along with his technical advisor A.S.E. Ackermann and British physicist Sir
      <a href="/wiki/C._V._Boys" title="C. V. Boys">
       Charles Vernon Boys
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
       <a href="#cite_note-21">
        [21]
       </a>
      </sup>
      developed an improved system using mirrors to reflect solar energy upon collector boxes, increasing heating capacity to the extent that water could now be used instead of ether. Shuman then constructed a full-scale steam engine powered by low-pressure water, enabling him to patent the entire solar engine system by 1912.
     </p>
     <p>
      Shuman built the world’s first
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_thermal_energy" title="Solar thermal energy">
       solar thermal power station
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Maadi" title="Maadi">
       Maadi
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">
       Egypt
      </a>
      , between 1912 and 1913. His plant used
      <a href="/wiki/Parabolic_trough" title="Parabolic trough">
       parabolic troughs
      </a>
      to power a 45–52 kilowatts (60–70
      <a href="/wiki/Horsepower" title="Horsepower">
       hp
      </a>
      ) engine that pumped more than 22,000 litres (4,800 imp gal; 5,800 US gal) of water per minute from the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nile_River" title="Nile River">
       Nile River
      </a>
      to adjacent cotton fields. Although the outbreak of
      <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">
       World War I
      </a>
      and the discovery of
      <a href="/wiki/Petroleum_industry" title="Petroleum industry">
       cheap oil
      </a>
      in the 1930s discouraged the advancement of solar energy, Shuman’s vision and basic design were resurrected in the 1970s with a new wave of interest in solar thermal energy.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
       <a href="#cite_note-22">
        [22]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1916 Shuman was quoted in the media advocating solar energy's utilization, saying:
     </p>
     <blockquote class="templatequote">
      <p>
       We have proved the commercial profit of sun power in the tropics and have more particularly proved that after our stores of oil and coal are exhausted the human race can receive unlimited power from the rays of the sun.
      </p>
      <div class="templatequotecite">
       <cite>
        — Frank Shuman, New York Times, 2 July 1916
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes.com_23-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-nytimes.com-23">
          [23]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </cite>
      </div>
     </blockquote>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Water_heating">
       Water heating
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main articles:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_hot_water" title="Solar hot water">
       Solar hot water
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_combisystem" title="Solar combisystem">
       Solar combisystem
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Twice_Cropped_Zonnecollectoren.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="867" data-file-width="838" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Twice_Cropped_Zonnecollectoren.JPG/170px-Twice_Cropped_Zonnecollectoren.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Twice_Cropped_Zonnecollectoren.JPG/255px-Twice_Cropped_Zonnecollectoren.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Twice_Cropped_Zonnecollectoren.JPG/340px-Twice_Cropped_Zonnecollectoren.JPG 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Twice_Cropped_Zonnecollectoren.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Solar water heaters facing the
        <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">
         Sun
        </a>
        to maximize gain
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Solar hot water systems use sunlight to heat water. In low geographical latitudes (below 40 degrees) from 60 to 70% of the domestic hot water use with temperatures up to 60 °C can be provided by solar heating systems.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
       <a href="#cite_note-24">
        [24]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The most common types of solar water heaters are evacuated tube collectors (44%) and glazed flat plate collectors (34%) generally used for domestic hot water; and unglazed plastic collectors (21%) used mainly to heat swimming pools.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
       <a href="#cite_note-25">
        [25]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      As of 2007, the total installed capacity of solar hot water systems was approximately 154
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GWth" title="GWth">
       thermal
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gigawatt" title="Gigawatt">
       gigawatt
      </a>
      (GW
      <sub>
       th
      </sub>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SWH_2008_26-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-SWH_2008-26">
        [26]
       </a>
      </sup>
      China is the world leader in their deployment with 70 GW
      <sub>
       th
      </sub>
      installed as of 2006 and a long-term goal of 210 GW
      <sub>
       th
      </sub>
      by 2020.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
       <a href="#cite_note-27">
        [27]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">
       Israel
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">
       Cyprus
      </a>
      are the per capita leaders in the use of solar hot water systems with over 90% of homes using them.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
       <a href="#cite_note-28">
        [28]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the United States, Canada, and Australia, heating swimming pools is the dominant application of solar hot water with an installed capacity of 18 GW
      <sub>
       th
      </sub>
      as of 2005.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEA_Solar_Thermal_19-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-IEA_Solar_Thermal-19">
        [19]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Heating.2C_cooling_and_ventilation">
       Heating, cooling and ventilation
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main articles:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_heating" title="Solar heating">
       Solar heating
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Thermal_mass" title="Thermal mass">
       Thermal mass
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_chimney" title="Solar chimney">
       Solar chimney
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_air_conditioning" title="Solar air conditioning">
       Solar air conditioning
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      In the United States,
      <a href="/wiki/HVAC" title="HVAC">
       heating, ventilation and air conditioning
      </a>
      (HVAC) systems account for 30% (4.65 EJ/yr) of the energy used in commercial buildings and nearly 50% (10.1 EJ/yr) of the energy used in residential buildings.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
       <a href="#cite_note-29">
        [29]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
       <a href="#cite_note-30">
        [30]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Solar heating, cooling and ventilation technologies can be used to offset a portion of this energy.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flipped_MIT_Solar_One_house.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="259" data-file-width="332" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Flipped_MIT_Solar_One_house.png/220px-Flipped_MIT_Solar_One_house.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Flipped_MIT_Solar_One_house.png/330px-Flipped_MIT_Solar_One_house.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8a/Flipped_MIT_Solar_One_house.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flipped_MIT_Solar_One_house.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">
         MIT
        </a>
        's Solar House #1, built in 1939 in the U.S., used
        <a href="/wiki/Seasonal_thermal_energy_storage" title="Seasonal thermal energy storage">
         seasonal thermal energy storage
        </a>
        for year-round heating.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Thermal mass is any material that can be used to store heat—heat from the Sun in the case of solar energy. Common thermal mass materials include stone, cement and water. Historically they have been used in arid climates or warm temperate regions to keep buildings cool by absorbing solar energy during the day and radiating stored heat to the cooler atmosphere at night. However, they can be used in cold temperate areas to maintain warmth as well. The size and placement of thermal mass depend on several factors such as climate, daylighting and shading conditions. When properly incorporated, thermal mass maintains space temperatures in a comfortable range and reduces the need for auxiliary heating and cooling equipment.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
       <a href="#cite_note-31">
        [31]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      A solar chimney (or thermal chimney, in this context) is a passive solar ventilation system composed of a vertical shaft connecting the interior and exterior of a building. As the chimney warms, the air inside is heated causing an
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Updraft" title="Updraft">
       updraft
      </a>
      that pulls air through the building. Performance can be improved by using glazing and thermal mass materials
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
       <a href="#cite_note-32">
        [32]
       </a>
      </sup>
      in a way that mimics greenhouses.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Deciduous" title="Deciduous">
       Deciduous
      </a>
      trees and plants have been promoted as a means of controlling solar heating and cooling. When planted on the southern side of a building in the northern hemisphere or the northern side in the southern hemisphere, their leaves provide shade during the summer, while the bare limbs allow light to pass during the winter.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
       <a href="#cite_note-33">
        [33]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Since bare, leafless trees shade 1/3 to 1/2 of incident solar radiation, there is a balance between the benefits of summer shading and the corresponding loss of winter heating.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
       <a href="#cite_note-34">
        [34]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In climates with significant heating loads, deciduous trees should not be planted on the Equator-facing side of a building because they will interfere with winter solar availability. They can, however, be used on the east and west sides to provide a degree of summer shading without appreciably affecting winter solar gain.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
       <a href="#cite_note-35">
        [35]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Cooking">
       Cooking
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_cooker" title="Solar cooker">
       Solar cooker
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Auroville_Solar_Bowl.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="651" data-file-width="1024" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Auroville_Solar_Bowl.JPG/220px-Auroville_Solar_Bowl.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Auroville_Solar_Bowl.JPG/330px-Auroville_Solar_Bowl.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Auroville_Solar_Bowl.JPG/440px-Auroville_Solar_Bowl.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Auroville_Solar_Bowl.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Parabolic dish produces steam for cooking, in
        <a href="/wiki/Auroville" title="Auroville">
         Auroville
        </a>
        , India
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Solar cookers use sunlight for cooking, drying and
      <a href="/wiki/Pasteurization" title="Pasteurization">
       pasteurization
      </a>
      . They can be grouped into three broad categories: box cookers, panel cookers and reflector cookers.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
       <a href="#cite_note-36">
        [36]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The simplest solar cooker is the box cooker first built by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Horace_de_Saussure" title="Horace de Saussure">
       Horace de Saussure
      </a>
      in 1767.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
       <a href="#cite_note-37">
        [37]
       </a>
      </sup>
      A basic box cooker consists of an insulated container with a transparent lid. It can be used effectively with partially overcast skies and will typically reach temperatures of 90–150 °C (194–302 °F).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
       <a href="#cite_note-38">
        [38]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Panel cookers use a reflective panel to direct sunlight onto an insulated container and reach temperatures comparable to box cookers. Reflector cookers use various concentrating geometries (dish, trough, Fresnel mirrors) to focus light on a cooking container. These cookers reach temperatures of 315 °C (599 °F) and above but require direct light to function properly and must be repositioned to track the Sun.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
       <a href="#cite_note-39">
        [39]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Process_heat">
       Process heat
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main articles:
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_pond" title="Solar pond">
       Solar pond
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Salt_evaporation_pond" title="Salt evaporation pond">
       Salt evaporation pond
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_furnace" title="Solar furnace">
       Solar furnace
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Solar concentrating technologies such as parabolic dish, trough and Scheffler reflectors can provide process heat for commercial and industrial applications. The first commercial system was the
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_Total_Energy_Project" title="Solar Total Energy Project">
       Solar Total Energy Project
      </a>
      (STEP) in Shenandoah, Georgia, USA where a field of 114 parabolic dishes provided 50% of the process heating, air conditioning and electrical requirements for a clothing factory. This grid-connected cogeneration system provided 400 kW of electricity plus thermal energy in the form of 401 kW steam and 468 kW chilled water, and had a one-hour peak load thermal storage.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
       <a href="#cite_note-40">
        [40]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Evaporation ponds are shallow pools that concentrate dissolved solids through
      <a href="/wiki/Evaporation" title="Evaporation">
       evaporation
      </a>
      . The use of evaporation ponds to obtain salt from seawater is one of the oldest applications of solar energy. Modern uses include concentrating brine solutions used in leach mining and removing dissolved solids from waste streams.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
       <a href="#cite_note-41">
        [41]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Clothes_line" title="Clothes line">
       Clothes lines
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clotheshorse" title="Clotheshorse">
       clotheshorses
      </a>
      , and clothes racks dry clothes through evaporation by wind and sunlight without consuming electricity or gas. In some states of the United States legislation protects the "right to dry" clothes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
       <a href="#cite_note-42">
        [42]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Unglazed transpired collectors (UTC) are perforated sun-facing walls used for preheating ventilation air. UTCs can raise the incoming air temperature up to 22 °C (40 °F) and deliver outlet temperatures of 45–60 °C (113–140 °F).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UTC_43-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-UTC-43">
        [43]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The short payback period of transpired collectors (3 to 12 years) makes them a more cost-effective alternative than glazed collection systems.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UTC_43-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-UTC-43">
        [43]
       </a>
      </sup>
      As of 2003, over 80 systems with a combined collector area of 35,000 square metres (380,000 sq ft) had been installed worldwide, including an 860 m
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      (9,300 sq ft) collector in
      <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rica" title="Costa Rica">
       Costa Rica
      </a>
      used for drying coffee beans and a 1,300 m
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      (14,000 sq ft) collector in
      <a href="/wiki/Coimbatore" title="Coimbatore">
       Coimbatore
      </a>
      , India, used for drying marigolds.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leon_2006_44-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Leon_2006-44">
        [44]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Water_treatment">
       Water treatment
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main articles:
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_still" title="Solar still">
       Solar still
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_water_disinfection" title="Solar water disinfection">
       Solar water disinfection
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_desalination" title="Solar desalination">
       Solar desalination
      </a>
      , and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_Powered_Desalination_Unit" title="Solar Powered Desalination Unit">
       Solar Powered Desalination Unit
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Indonesia-sodis-gross.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Indonesia-sodis-gross.jpg/220px-Indonesia-sodis-gross.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Indonesia-sodis-gross.jpg/330px-Indonesia-sodis-gross.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Indonesia-sodis-gross.jpg/440px-Indonesia-sodis-gross.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Indonesia-sodis-gross.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Solar_water_disinfection" title="Solar water disinfection">
         Solar water disinfection
        </a>
        in Indonesia
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Solar distillation can be used to make
      <a href="/wiki/Saline_water" title="Saline water">
       saline
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Brackish_water" title="Brackish water">
       brackish water
      </a>
      potable. The first recorded instance of this was by 16th-century Arab alchemists.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tiwari_2003_45-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Tiwari_2003-45">
        [45]
       </a>
      </sup>
      A large-scale solar distillation project was first constructed in 1872 in the
      <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">
       Chilean
      </a>
      mining town of Las Salinas.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Daniels_1964_46-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Daniels_1964-46">
        [46]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The plant, which had solar collection area of 4,700 m
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      (51,000 sq ft), could produce up to 22,700 L (5,000 imp gal; 6,000 US gal) per day and operate for 40 years.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Daniels_1964_46-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Daniels_1964-46">
        [46]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Individual
      <a href="/wiki/Still" title="Still">
       still
      </a>
      designs include single-slope, double-slope (or greenhouse type), vertical, conical, inverted absorber, multi-wick, and multiple effect. These stills can operate in passive, active, or hybrid modes. Double-slope stills are the most economical for decentralized domestic purposes, while active multiple effect units are more suitable for large-scale applications.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tiwari_2003_45-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Tiwari_2003-45">
        [45]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Solar water
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Disinfection" title="Disinfection">
       disinfection
      </a>
      (SODIS) involves exposing water-filled plastic
      <a href="/wiki/Polyethylene_terephthalate" title="Polyethylene terephthalate">
       polyethylene terephthalate
      </a>
      (PET) bottles to sunlight for several hours.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
       <a href="#cite_note-47">
        [47]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Exposure times vary depending on weather and climate from a minimum of six hours to two days during fully overcast conditions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SODIS_CDC_48-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-SODIS_CDC-48">
        [48]
       </a>
      </sup>
      It is recommended by the
      <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">
       World Health Organization
      </a>
      as a viable method for household water treatment and safe storage.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
       <a href="#cite_note-49">
        [49]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Over two million people in developing countries use this method for their daily drinking water.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SODIS_CDC_48-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-SODIS_CDC-48">
        [48]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Solar energy may be used in a water stabilization pond to treat
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Waste_water" title="Waste water">
       waste water
      </a>
      without chemicals or electricity. A further environmental advantage is that
      <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">
       algae
      </a>
      grow in such ponds and consume
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">
       carbon dioxide
      </a>
      in photosynthesis, although algae may produce toxic chemicals that make the water unusable.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
       <a href="#cite_note-50">
        [50]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
       <a href="#cite_note-51">
        [51]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Electricity_production">
       Electricity production
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_power" title="Solar power">
       Solar power
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tmulti tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:458px;max-width:458px">
       <div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:227px;max-width:227px">
        <div class="thumbimage">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:IvanpahRunning.JPG">
          <img alt="" data-file-height="3264" data-file-width="4912" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/IvanpahRunning.JPG/225px-IvanpahRunning.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/IvanpahRunning.JPG/338px-IvanpahRunning.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/IvanpahRunning.JPG/450px-IvanpahRunning.JPG 2x" width="225"/>
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
       <div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:227px;max-width:227px">
        <div class="thumbimage">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Topaz_Solar_Farm,_California_Valley.jpg">
          <img alt="" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="720" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Topaz_Solar_Farm%2C_California_Valley.jpg/225px-Topaz_Solar_Farm%2C_California_Valley.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Topaz_Solar_Farm%2C_California_Valley.jpg/338px-Topaz_Solar_Farm%2C_California_Valley.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Topaz_Solar_Farm%2C_California_Valley.jpg/450px-Topaz_Solar_Farm%2C_California_Valley.jpg 2x" width="225"/>
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
       <div style="clear:left">
       </div>
       <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">
        Some of the world's largest solar power stations:
        <a href="/wiki/Ivanpah_Solar_Power_Facility" title="Ivanpah Solar Power Facility">
         Ivanpah
        </a>
        (CSP) and
        <a href="/wiki/Topaz_Solar_Farm" title="Topaz Solar Farm">
         Topaz
        </a>
        (PV)
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Solar power is the conversion of sunlight into
      <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">
       electricity
      </a>
      , either directly using
      <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaics" title="Photovoltaics">
       photovoltaics
      </a>
      (PV), or indirectly using
      <a href="/wiki/Concentrated_solar_power" title="Concentrated solar power">
       concentrated solar power
      </a>
      (CSP). CSP systems use lenses or mirrors and tracking systems to focus a large area of sunlight into a small beam. PV converts light into electric current using the
      <a href="/wiki/Photoelectric_effect" title="Photoelectric effect">
       photoelectric effect
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Solar power is anticipated to become the world's largest source of electricity by 2050, with solar photovoltaics and concentrated solar power contributing 16 and 11 percent to the global overall consumption, respectively.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
       <a href="#cite_note-52">
        [52]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Commercial CSP plants were first developed in the 1980s. Since 1985 the eventually 354 MW
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_Energy_Generating_Systems" title="Solar Energy Generating Systems">
       SEGS
      </a>
      CSP installation, in the Mojave Desert of California, is the largest solar power plant in the world. Other large CSP plants include the 150 MW
      <a href="/wiki/Solnova_Solar_Power_Station" title="Solnova Solar Power Station">
       Solnova Solar Power Station
      </a>
      and the 100 MW
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Andasol_solar_power_station" title="Andasol solar power station">
       Andasol solar power station
      </a>
      , both in Spain. The 250 MW
      <a href="/wiki/Agua_Caliente_Solar_Project" title="Agua Caliente Solar Project">
       Agua Caliente Solar Project
      </a>
      , in the United States, and the 221 MW
      <a href="/wiki/Gujarat_Solar_Park" title="Gujarat Solar Park">
       Charanka Solar Park
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">
       India
      </a>
      , are the
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_photovoltaic_power_stations" title="List of photovoltaic power stations">
       world’s largest
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_plant" title="Photovoltaic plant">
       photovoltaic plants
      </a>
      . Solar projects exceeding 1 GW are being developed, but most of the deployed photovoltaics are in small rooftop arrays of less than 5 kW, which are connected to the grid using net metering and/or a feed-in tariff.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
       <a href="#cite_note-53">
        [53]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 2013 solar generated less than 1% of the world's total grid electricity.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
       <a href="#cite_note-54">
        [54]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>

      </a>
     </div>
