        <div class="magnify" style="float:right">
         <a href="/wiki/Growth_of_photovoltaics" title="Growth of photovoltaics">
          <img alt="Desc-i.svg" data-file-height="648" data-file-width="648" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Desc-i.svg/16px-Desc-i.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Desc-i.svg/24px-Desc-i.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Desc-i.svg/32px-Desc-i.svg.png 2x" width="16"/>
         </a>
        </div>
        <div style="background-color: none; line-height: 1.3em;">
         <div style="margin-bottom: 1px; width: 38%; float: left; padding-left: 8%;">
          <span style="margin:0px; font-size:90%;">
           <span style="border:1px solid #134E13; background-color:forestgreen; color:forestgreen;">
           </span>
           Europe
          </span>
         </div>
         <div style="margin-bottom: 1px; width: 42%; float: left;">
          <span style="margin:0px; font-size:90%;">
           <span style="border:1px solid #B37400; background-color:orange; color:orange;">
           </span>
           Asia-Pacific
          </span>
         </div>
         <div style="margin-bottom: 1px; width: 38%; float: left; padding-left: 8%;">
          <span style="margin:0px; font-size:90%;">
           <span style="border:1px solid navy; background-color:#2E5CB8; color:#2E5CB8;">
           </span>
           Americas
          </span>
         </div>
         <div style="margin-bottom: 1px; width: 42%; float: left;">
          <span style="margin:0px; font-size:90%;">
           <span style="border:1px solid #AD102F; background-color:crimson; color:crimson;">
           </span>
           China
          </span>
         </div>
         <div style="margin-bottom: 1px; clear: both; padding-left: 8%;">
          <span style="margin:0px; font-size:90%;">
           <span style="border:1px solid #B3B300; background-color:yellow; color:yellow;">
           </span>
           Middle East and Africa
          </span>
         </div>
         <p>
          <a href="/wiki/Growth_of_photovoltaics" title="Growth of photovoltaics">
           Worldwide growth
          </a>
          of PV capacity grouped by region in MW (2006–2014)
         </p>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In the last two decades,
      <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaics" title="Photovoltaics">
       photovoltaics
      </a>
      (PV), also known as solar PV, has evolved from a pure niche market of small scale applications towards becoming a mainstream electricity source. A
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_cell" title="Solar cell">
       solar cell
      </a>
      is a device that converts light directly into electricity using the photoelectric effect. The first solar cell was constructed by
      <a href="/wiki/Charles_Fritts" title="Charles Fritts">
       Charles Fritts
      </a>
      in the 1880s.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
       <a href="#cite_note-55">
        [55]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1931 a German engineer, Dr Bruno Lange, developed a photo cell using
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silver_selenide" title="Silver selenide">
       silver selenide
      </a>
      in place of
      <a href="/wiki/Copper_oxide" title="Copper oxide">
       copper oxide
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
       <a href="#cite_note-56">
        [56]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Although the prototype
      <a href="/wiki/Selenium" title="Selenium">
       selenium
      </a>
      cells converted less than 1% of incident light into electricity, both
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ernst_Werner_von_Siemens" title="Ernst Werner von Siemens">
       Ernst Werner von Siemens
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/James_Clerk_Maxwell" title="James Clerk Maxwell">
       James Clerk Maxwell
      </a>
      recognized the importance of this discovery.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
       <a href="#cite_note-57">
        [57]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Following the work of
      <a href="/wiki/Russell_Ohl" title="Russell Ohl">
       Russell Ohl
      </a>
      in the 1940s, researchers Gerald Pearson,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calvin_Fuller" title="Calvin Fuller">
       Calvin Fuller
      </a>
      and Daryl Chapin created the
      <a href="/wiki/Crystalline_silicon" title="Crystalline silicon">
       crystalline silicon
      </a>
      solar cell in 1954.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
       <a href="#cite_note-58">
        [58]
       </a>
      </sup>
      These early solar cells cost 286 USD/watt and reached efficiencies of 4.5–6%.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
       <a href="#cite_note-59">
        [59]
       </a>
      </sup>
      By 2012 available efficiencies exceeded 20%, and the maximum efficiency of research photovoltaics was in excess of 40%.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
       <a href="#cite_note-60">
        [60]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Concentrated_solar_power">
       Concentrated solar power
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Concentrated_solar_power" title="Concentrated solar power">
       Concentrated solar power
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Concentrating Solar Power (CSP) systems use lenses or mirrors and tracking systems to focus a large area of sunlight into a small beam. The concentrated heat is then used as a heat source for a conventional power plant. A wide range of concentrating technologies exists; the most developed are the parabolic trough, the concentrating linear fresnel reflector, the Stirling dish and the solar power tower. Various techniques are used to track the Sun and focus light. In all of these systems a
      <a href="/wiki/Working_fluid" title="Working fluid">
       working fluid
      </a>
      is heated by the concentrated sunlight, and is then used for power generation or energy storage.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
       <a href="#cite_note-61">
        [61]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture_and_urban_planning">
       Architecture and urban planning
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main articles:
      <a href="/wiki/Passive_solar_building_design" title="Passive solar building design">
       Passive solar building design
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Urban_heat_island" title="Urban heat island">
       Urban heat island
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Technische_Universit%C3%A4t_Darmstadt_-_Solar_Decathlon_2007.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1998" data-file-width="3024" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Technische_Universit%C3%A4t_Darmstadt_-_Solar_Decathlon_2007.jpg/220px-Technische_Universit%C3%A4t_Darmstadt_-_Solar_Decathlon_2007.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Technische_Universit%C3%A4t_Darmstadt_-_Solar_Decathlon_2007.jpg/330px-Technische_Universit%C3%A4t_Darmstadt_-_Solar_Decathlon_2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Technische_Universit%C3%A4t_Darmstadt_-_Solar_Decathlon_2007.jpg/440px-Technische_Universit%C3%A4t_Darmstadt_-_Solar_Decathlon_2007.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Technische_Universit%C3%A4t_Darmstadt_-_Solar_Decathlon_2007.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darmstadt_University_of_Technology" title="Darmstadt University of Technology">
         Darmstadt University of Technology
        </a>
        , Germany, won the 2007
        <a href="/wiki/Solar_Decathlon" title="Solar Decathlon">
         Solar Decathlon
        </a>
        in Washington, D.C. with this
        <a href="/wiki/Passive_house" title="Passive house">
         passive house
        </a>
        designed for humid and hot subtropical climate.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
         <a href="#cite_note-62">
          [62]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Sunlight has influenced building design since the beginning of architectural history.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schittich_2003_63-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Schittich_2003-63">
        [63]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Advanced solar architecture and urban planning methods were first employed by the
      <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">
       Greeks
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Feng_shui#Archaeology" title="Feng shui">
       Chinese
      </a>
      , who oriented their buildings toward the south to provide light and warmth.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
       <a href="#cite_note-64">
        [64]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The common features of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Passive_solar" title="Passive solar">
       passive solar
      </a>
      architecture are orientation relative to the Sun, compact proportion (a low surface area to volume ratio), selective shading (overhangs) and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermal_mass_(Building)" title="Thermal mass (Building)">
       thermal mass
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schittich_2003_63-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Schittich_2003-63">
        [63]
       </a>
      </sup>
      When these features are tailored to the local climate and environment they can produce well-lit spaces that stay in a comfortable temperature range.
      <a href="/wiki/Socrates" title="Socrates">
       Socrates
      </a>
      ' Megaron House is a classic example of passive solar design.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schittich_2003_63-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Schittich_2003-63">
        [63]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The most recent approaches to solar design use computer modeling tying together
      <a href="/wiki/Daylighting" title="Daylighting">
       solar lighting
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_heating" title="Solar heating">
       heating
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_air_conditioning" title="Solar air conditioning">
       ventilation
      </a>
      systems in an integrated
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_design" title="Solar design">
       solar design
      </a>
      package.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
       <a href="#cite_note-65">
        [65]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Active_solar" title="Active solar">
       Active solar
      </a>
      equipment such as pumps, fans and switchable windows can complement passive design and improve system performance.
     </p>
     <p>
      Urban heat islands (UHI) are metropolitan areas with higher temperatures than that of the surrounding environment. The higher temperatures result from increased absorption of solar energy by urban materials such as asphalt and concrete, which have lower
      <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">
       albedos
      </a>
      and higher
      <a href="/wiki/Heat_capacity" title="Heat capacity">
       heat capacities
      </a>
      than those in the natural environment. A straightforward method of counteracting the UHI effect is to paint buildings and roads white, and to plant trees in the area. Using these methods, a hypothetical "cool communities" program in
      <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">
       Los Angeles
      </a>
      has projected that urban temperatures could be reduced by approximately 3 °C at an estimated cost of US$1 billion, giving estimated total annual benefits of US$530 million from reduced air-conditioning costs and healthcare savings.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
       <a href="#cite_note-66">
        [66]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Agriculture_and_horticulture">
       Agriculture and horticulture
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Westland_kassen.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="488" data-file-width="1576" height="68" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Westland_kassen.jpg/220px-Westland_kassen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Westland_kassen.jpg/330px-Westland_kassen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Westland_kassen.jpg/440px-Westland_kassen.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Westland_kassen.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greenhouses" title="Greenhouses">
         Greenhouses
        </a>
        like these in the Westland municipality of the
        <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">
         Netherlands
        </a>
        grow vegetables, fruits and flowers.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">
       Agriculture
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Horticulture" title="Horticulture">
       horticulture
      </a>
      seek to optimize the capture of solar energy in order to optimize the productivity of plants. Techniques such as timed planting cycles, tailored row orientation, staggered heights between rows and the mixing of plant varieties can improve crop yields.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
       <a href="#cite_note-67">
        [67]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
       <a href="#cite_note-68">
        [68]
       </a>
      </sup>
      While sunlight is generally considered a plentiful resource, the exceptions highlight the importance of solar energy to agriculture. During the short growing seasons of the
      <a href="/wiki/Little_Ice_Age" title="Little Ice Age">
       Little Ice Age
      </a>
      , French and
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Solar power in the United Kingdom">
       English
      </a>
      farmers employed fruit walls to maximize the collection of solar energy. These walls acted as thermal masses and accelerated ripening by keeping plants warm. Early fruit walls were built perpendicular to the ground and facing south, but over time, sloping walls were developed to make better use of sunlight. In 1699,
      <a href="/wiki/Nicolas_Fatio_de_Duillier" title="Nicolas Fatio de Duillier">
       Nicolas Fatio de Duillier
      </a>
      even suggested using a
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_tracker" title="Solar tracker">
       tracking mechanism
      </a>
      which could pivot to follow the Sun.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
       <a href="#cite_note-69">
        [69]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Applications of solar energy in agriculture aside from growing crops include pumping water, drying crops, brooding chicks and drying chicken manure.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leon_2006_44-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Leon_2006-44">
        [44]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
       <a href="#cite_note-70">
        [70]
       </a>
      </sup>
      More recently the technology has been embraced by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Winemakers" title="Winemakers">
       vintners
      </a>
      , who use the energy generated by solar panels to power grape presses.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
       <a href="#cite_note-71">
        [71]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse" title="Greenhouse">
       Greenhouses
      </a>
      convert solar light to heat, enabling year-round production and the growth (in enclosed environments) of specialty crops and other plants not naturally suited to the local climate. Primitive greenhouses were first used during Roman times to produce
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cucumbers" title="Cucumbers">
       cucumbers
      </a>
      year-round for the Roman emperor
      <a href="/wiki/Tiberius" title="Tiberius">
       Tiberius
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
       <a href="#cite_note-72">
        [72]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The first modern greenhouses were built in Europe in the 16th century to keep exotic plants brought back from explorations abroad.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
       <a href="#cite_note-73">
        [73]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Greenhouses remain an important part of horticulture today, and plastic transparent materials have also been used to similar effect in
      <a href="/wiki/Polytunnel" title="Polytunnel">
       polytunnels
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Row_cover" title="Row cover">
       row covers
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Transport">
       Transport
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main articles:
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_vehicle" title="Solar vehicle">
       Solar vehicle
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar-charged_vehicle" title="Solar-charged vehicle">
       Solar-charged vehicle
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Electric_boat" title="Electric boat">
       Electric boat
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_balloon" title="Solar balloon">
       Solar balloon
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tmulti tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:229px;max-width:229px">
       <div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:227px;max-width:227px">
        <div class="thumbimage">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nuna_7.jpg">
          <img alt="" data-file-height="2204" data-file-width="3920" height="127" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Nuna_7.jpg/225px-Nuna_7.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Nuna_7.jpg/338px-Nuna_7.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Nuna_7.jpg/450px-Nuna_7.jpg 2x" width="225"/>
         </a>
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
         <a href="/wiki/Nuna_7" title="Nuna 7">
          Winner
         </a>
         of the 2013
         <a href="/wiki/World_Solar_Challenge" title="World Solar Challenge">
          World Solar Challenge
         </a>
         in Australia
        </div>
       </div>
       <div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:227px;max-width:227px">
        <div class="thumbimage">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flea_Hop_HB-SIA_-_Solar_Impulse.jpg">
          <img alt="" data-file-height="2238" data-file-width="3543" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Flea_Hop_HB-SIA_-_Solar_Impulse.jpg/225px-Flea_Hop_HB-SIA_-_Solar_Impulse.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Flea_Hop_HB-SIA_-_Solar_Impulse.jpg/338px-Flea_Hop_HB-SIA_-_Solar_Impulse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Flea_Hop_HB-SIA_-_Solar_Impulse.jpg/450px-Flea_Hop_HB-SIA_-_Solar_Impulse.jpg 2x" width="225"/>
         </a>
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
         Solar
         <a href="/wiki/Electric_aircraft" title="Electric aircraft">
          electric aircraft
         </a>
         circumnavigating the globe in 2015
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Development of a solar-powered car has been an engineering goal since the 1980s. The
      <a href="/wiki/World_Solar_Challenge" title="World Solar Challenge">
       World Solar Challenge
      </a>
      is a biannual solar-powered car race, where teams from universities and enterprises compete over 3,021 kilometres (1,877 mi) across central Australia from
      <a href="/wiki/Darwin,_Northern_Territory" title="Darwin, Northern Territory">
       Darwin
      </a>
      to
      <a href="/wiki/Adelaide" title="Adelaide">
       Adelaide
      </a>
      . In 1987, when it was founded, the winner's average speed was 67 kilometres per hour (42 mph) and by 2007 the winner's average speed had improved to 90.87 kilometres per hour (56.46 mph).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
       <a href="#cite_note-74">
        [74]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_American_Solar_Challenge" title="North American Solar Challenge">
       North American Solar Challenge
      </a>
      and the planned
      <a href="/wiki/South_African_Solar_Challenge" title="South African Solar Challenge">
       South African Solar Challenge
      </a>
      are comparable competitions that reflect an international interest in the engineering and development of solar powered vehicles.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
       <a href="#cite_note-75">
        [75]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
       <a href="#cite_note-76">
        [76]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Some vehicles use solar panels for auxiliary power, such as for air conditioning, to keep the interior cool, thus reducing fuel consumption.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
       <a href="#cite_note-77">
        [77]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
       <a href="#cite_note-78">
        [78]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1975, the first practical solar boat was constructed in England.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
       <a href="#cite_note-79">
        [79]
       </a>
      </sup>
      By 1995, passenger boats incorporating PV panels began appearing and are now used extensively.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
       <a href="#cite_note-80">
        [80]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1996,
      <a href="/wiki/Kenichi_Horie" title="Kenichi Horie">
       Kenichi Horie
      </a>
      made the first solar-powered crossing of the Pacific Ocean, and the
      <i>
       Sun21
      </i>
      catamaran made the first solar-powered crossing of the Atlantic Ocean in the winter of 2006–2007.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
       <a href="#cite_note-81">
        [81]
       </a>
      </sup>
      There were plans to circumnavigate the globe in 2010.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
       <a href="#cite_note-82">
        [82]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1974, the unmanned
      <a href="/wiki/AstroFlight_Sunrise" title="AstroFlight Sunrise">
       AstroFlight Sunrise
      </a>
      airplane made the first solar flight. On 29 April 1979, the
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_Riser" title="Solar Riser">
        Solar Riser
       </a>
      </i>
      made the first flight in a solar-powered, fully controlled, man-carrying flying machine, reaching an altitude of 40 feet (12 m). In 1980, the
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gossamer_Albatross#Solar-powered_variants" title="Gossamer Albatross">
        Gossamer Penguin
       </a>
      </i>
      made the first piloted flights powered solely by photovoltaics. This was quickly followed by the
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_Challenger" title="Solar Challenger">
        Solar Challenger
       </a>
      </i>
      which crossed the English Channel in July 1981. In 1990
      <a href="/wiki/Eric_Scott_Raymond" title="Eric Scott Raymond">
       Eric Scott Raymond
      </a>
      in 21 hops flew from California to North Carolina using solar power.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
       <a href="#cite_note-83">
        [83]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Developments then turned back to unmanned aerial vehicles (UAV) with the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/NASA_Pathfinder" title="NASA Pathfinder">
        Pathfinder
       </a>
      </i>
      (1997) and subsequent designs, culminating in the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Helios_Prototype" title="Helios Prototype">
        Helios
       </a>
      </i>
      which set the altitude record for a non-rocket-propelled aircraft at 29,524 metres (96,864 ft) in 2001.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
       <a href="#cite_note-84">
        [84]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/QinetiQ_Zephyr" title="QinetiQ Zephyr">
        Zephyr
       </a>
      </i>
      , developed by
      <a href="/wiki/BAE_Systems" title="BAE Systems">
       BAE Systems
      </a>
      , is the latest in a line of record-breaking solar aircraft, making a 54-hour flight in 2007, and month-long flights were envisioned by 2010.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
       <a href="#cite_note-85">
        [85]
       </a>
      </sup>
      As of 2016,
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_Impulse" title="Solar Impulse">
       Solar Impulse
      </a>
      , an
      <a href="/wiki/Electric_aircraft" title="Electric aircraft">
       electric aircraft
      </a>
      , is currently circumnavigating the globe. It is a single-seat plane powered by
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_cell" title="Solar cell">
       solar cells
      </a>
      and capable of taking off under its own power. The design allows the aircraft to remain airborne for several days.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
       <a href="#cite_note-86">
        [86]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      A
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_balloon" title="Solar balloon">
       solar balloon
      </a>
      is a black balloon that is filled with ordinary air. As sunlight shines on the balloon, the air inside is heated and expands causing an upward
      <a href="/wiki/Buoyancy" title="Buoyancy">
       buoyancy
      </a>
      force, much like an artificially heated
      <a href="/wiki/Hot_air_balloon" title="Hot air balloon">
       hot air balloon
      </a>
      . Some solar balloons are large enough for human flight, but usage is generally limited to the toy market as the surface-area to payload-weight ratio is relatively high.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
       <a href="#cite_note-87">
        [87]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Fuel_production">
       Fuel production
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Photo_of_the_Week-_Boosting_Solar_Technology_(8722948189).jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="777" data-file-width="1042" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Photo_of_the_Week-_Boosting_Solar_Technology_%288722948189%29.jpg/220px-Photo_of_the_Week-_Boosting_Solar_Technology_%288722948189%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Photo_of_the_Week-_Boosting_Solar_Technology_%288722948189%29.jpg/330px-Photo_of_the_Week-_Boosting_Solar_Technology_%288722948189%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Photo_of_the_Week-_Boosting_Solar_Technology_%288722948189%29.jpg/440px-Photo_of_the_Week-_Boosting_Solar_Technology_%288722948189%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Photo_of_the_Week-_Boosting_Solar_Technology_(8722948189).jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Concentrated solar panels are getting a power boost.
        <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Northwest_National_Laboratory" title="Pacific Northwest National Laboratory">
         Pacific Northwest National Laboratory
        </a>
        (PNNL) will be testing a new concentrated solar power system -- one that can help natural gas power plants reduce their fuel usage by up to 20 percent.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main articles:
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_chemical" title="Solar chemical">
       Solar chemical
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_fuel" title="Solar fuel">
       Solar fuel
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Artificial_photosynthesis" title="Artificial photosynthesis">
       Artificial photosynthesis
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Solar chemical processes use solar energy to drive chemical reactions. These processes offset energy that would otherwise come from a fossil fuel source and can also convert solar energy into storable and transportable fuels. Solar induced chemical reactions can be divided into thermochemical or
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photochemical" title="Photochemical">
       photochemical
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
       <a href="#cite_note-88">
        [88]
       </a>
      </sup>
      A variety of fuels can be produced by
      <a href="/wiki/Artificial_photosynthesis" title="Artificial photosynthesis">
       artificial photosynthesis
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
       <a href="#cite_note-89">
        [89]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The multielectron catalytic chemistry involved in making carbon-based fuels (such as
      <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">
       methanol
      </a>
      ) from reduction of
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">
       carbon dioxide
      </a>
      is challenging; a feasible alternative is
      <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">
       hydrogen
      </a>
      production from protons, though use of water as the source of electrons (as plants do) requires mastering the multielectron oxidation of two water molecules to molecular oxygen.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
       <a href="#cite_note-90">
        [90]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some have envisaged working solar fuel plants in coastal metropolitan areas by 2050 –  the splitting of sea water providing hydrogen to be run through adjacent fuel-cell electric power plants and the pure water by-product going directly into the municipal water system.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
       <a href="#cite_note-91">
        [91]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Another vision involves all human structures covering the earth's surface (i.e., roads, vehicles and buildings) doing photosynthesis more efficiently than plants.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">
       <a href="#cite_note-92">
        [92]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_production" title="Hydrogen production">
       Hydrogen production
      </a>
      technologies have been a significant area of solar chemical research since the 1970s. Aside from electrolysis driven by photovoltaic or photochemical cells, several thermochemical processes have also been explored. One such route uses concentrators to split water into oxygen and hydrogen at high temperatures (2,300–2,600 °C or 4,200–4,700 °F).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">
       <a href="#cite_note-93">
        [93]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Another approach uses the heat from solar concentrators to drive the
      <a href="/wiki/Steam_reforming" title="Steam reforming">
       steam reformation
      </a>
      of natural gas thereby increasing the overall hydrogen yield compared to conventional reforming methods.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">
       <a href="#cite_note-94">
        [94]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Thermochemical cycles characterized by the decomposition and regeneration of reactants present another avenue for hydrogen production. The Solzinc process under development at the
      <a href="/wiki/Weizmann_Institute_of_Science" title="Weizmann Institute of Science">
       Weizmann Institute of Science
      </a>
      uses a 1 MW solar furnace to decompose
      <a href="/wiki/Zinc_oxide" title="Zinc oxide">
       zinc oxide
      </a>
      (ZnO) at temperatures above 1,200 °C (2,200 °F). This initial reaction produces pure zinc, which can subsequently be reacted with water to produce hydrogen.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95">
       <a href="#cite_note-95">
        [95]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_storage_methods">
       Energy storage methods
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main articles:
      <a href="/wiki/Energy_storage" title="Energy storage">
       Energy storage
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Seasonal_thermal_energy_storage" title="Seasonal thermal energy storage">
       Seasonal thermal energy storage
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phase_change_material" title="Phase change material">
       Phase change material
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Grid_energy_storage" title="Grid energy storage">
       Grid energy storage
      </a>
      , and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/V2G" title="V2G">
       V2G
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:12-05-08_AS1.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2336" data-file-width="3504" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/12-05-08_AS1.JPG/220px-12-05-08_AS1.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/12-05-08_AS1.JPG/330px-12-05-08_AS1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/12-05-08_AS1.JPG/440px-12-05-08_AS1.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:12-05-08_AS1.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Thermal_energy_storage" title="Thermal energy storage">
         Thermal energy storage
        </a>
        . The
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Andasol_solar_power_station" title="Andasol solar power station">
         Andasol
        </a>
        CSP plant uses tanks of molten salt to store solar energy.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Thermal_mass" title="Thermal mass">
       Thermal mass
      </a>
      systems can store solar energy in the form of heat at domestically useful temperatures for daily or
      <a href="/wiki/Seasonal_thermal_energy_storage" title="Seasonal thermal energy storage">
       interseasonal durations
      </a>
      . Thermal storage systems generally use readily available materials with high
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Specific_heat" title="Specific heat">
       specific heat
      </a>
      capacities such as water, earth and stone. Well-designed systems can lower
      <a href="/wiki/Peak_demand" title="Peak demand">
       peak demand
      </a>
      , shift time-of-use to
      <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/off-peak" title="wikt:off-peak">
       off-peak
      </a>
      hours and reduce overall heating and cooling requirements.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96">
       <a href="#cite_note-96">
        [96]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97">
       <a href="#cite_note-97">
        [97]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Phase change materials such as
      <a href="/wiki/Paraffin_wax" title="Paraffin wax">
       paraffin wax
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Sodium_sulfate#Thermal_storage" title="Sodium sulfate">
       Glauber's salt
      </a>
      are another thermal storage medium. These materials are inexpensive, readily available, and can deliver domestically useful temperatures (approximately 64 °C or 147 °F). The "Dover House" (in
      <a href="/wiki/Dover,_Massachusetts" title="Dover, Massachusetts">
       Dover, Massachusetts
      </a>
      ) was the first to use a Glauber's salt heating system, in 1948.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98">
       <a href="#cite_note-98">
        [98]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Solar energy can also be stored at high temperatures using
      <a href="/wiki/Molten_salt" title="Molten salt">
       molten salts
      </a>
      . Salts are an effective storage medium because they are low-cost, have a high specific heat capacity and can deliver heat at temperatures compatible with conventional power systems. The
      <a href="/wiki/The_Solar_Project#Solar_Two" title="The Solar Project">
       Solar Two
      </a>
      project used this method of energy storage, allowing it to store 1.44
      <a href="/wiki/Joule" title="Joule">
       terajoules
      </a>
      (400,000 kWh) in its 68 m³ storage tank with an annual storage efficiency of about 99%.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99">
       <a href="#cite_note-99">
        [99]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Off-grid
      <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaics" title="Photovoltaics">
       PV systems
      </a>
      have traditionally used
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rechargeable_batteries" title="Rechargeable batteries">
       rechargeable batteries
      </a>
      to store excess electricity. With grid-tied systems, excess electricity can be sent to the transmission
      <a href="/wiki/Grid-tied_electrical_system" title="Grid-tied electrical system">
       grid
      </a>
      , while standard grid electricity can be used to meet shortfalls.
      <a href="/wiki/Net_metering" title="Net metering">
       Net metering
      </a>
      programs give household systems a credit for any electricity they deliver to the grid. This is handled by 'rolling back' the meter whenever the home produces more electricity than it consumes. If the net electricity use is below zero, the utility then rolls over the kilowatt hour credit to the next month.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100">
       <a href="#cite_note-100">
        [100]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Other approaches involve the use of two meters, to measure electricity consumed vs. electricity produced. This is less common due to the increased installation cost of the second meter. Most standard meters accurately measure in both directions, making a second meter unnecessary.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Pumped-storage_hydroelectricity" title="Pumped-storage hydroelectricity">
       Pumped-storage hydroelectricity
      </a>
      stores energy in the form of water pumped when energy is available from a lower elevation reservoir to a higher elevation one. The energy is recovered when demand is high by releasing the water, with the pump becoming a hydroelectric power generator.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101">
       <a href="#cite_note-101">
        [101]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Development.2C_deployment_and_economics">
       Development, deployment and economics
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:03242012Taller_sostenibilidad_lore060.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1872" data-file-width="2812" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/03242012Taller_sostenibilidad_lore060.jpg/220px-03242012Taller_sostenibilidad_lore060.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/03242012Taller_sostenibilidad_lore060.jpg/330px-03242012Taller_sostenibilidad_lore060.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/03242012Taller_sostenibilidad_lore060.jpg/440px-03242012Taller_sostenibilidad_lore060.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:03242012Taller_sostenibilidad_lore060.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Participants in a workshop on sustainable development inspect solar panels at
        <a href="/wiki/Monterrey_Institute_of_Technology_and_Higher_Education,_Mexico_City" title="Monterrey Institute of Technology and Higher Education, Mexico City">
         Monterrey Institute of Technology and Higher Education, Mexico City
        </a>
        on top of a building on campus.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deployment_of_solar_power_to_energy_grids" title="Deployment of solar power to energy grids">
       Deployment of solar power to energy grids
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Cost_of_electricity_by_source" title="Cost of electricity by source">
       Cost of electricity by source
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Beginning with the surge in
      <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">
       coal
      </a>
      use which accompanied the
      <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">
       Industrial Revolution
      </a>
      , energy consumption has steadily transitioned from wood and biomass to
      <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">
       fossil fuels
      </a>
      . The early development of solar technologies starting in the 1860s was driven by an expectation that coal would soon become scarce. However, development of solar technologies stagnated in the early 20th century in the face of the increasing availability, economy, and utility of coal and
      <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">
       petroleum
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102">
       <a href="#cite_note-102">
        [102]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/1973_oil_crisis" title="1973 oil crisis">
       1973 oil embargo
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/1979_energy_crisis" title="1979 energy crisis">
       1979 energy crisis
      </a>
      caused a reorganization of energy policies around the world and brought renewed attention to developing solar technologies.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103">
       <a href="#cite_note-103">
        [103]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104">
       <a href="#cite_note-104">
        [104]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Deployment strategies focused on incentive programs such as the Federal Photovoltaic Utilization Program in the U.S. and the Sunshine Program in Japan. Other efforts included the formation of research facilities in the U.S. (SERI, now
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NREL" title="NREL">
       NREL
      </a>
      ), Japan (
      <a href="/wiki/New_Energy_and_Industrial_Technology_Development_Organization" title="New Energy and Industrial Technology Development Organization">
       NEDO
      </a>
      ), and
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Germany" title="Solar power in Germany">
       Germany
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Fraunhofer_Society" title="Fraunhofer Society">
       Fraunhofer Institute for Solar Energy Systems ISE
      </a>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105">
       <a href="#cite_note-105">
        [105]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Commercial solar water heaters began appearing in the United States in the 1890s.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106">
       <a href="#cite_note-106">
        [106]
       </a>
      </sup>
      These systems saw increasing use until the 1920s but were gradually replaced by cheaper and more reliable heating fuels.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107">
       <a href="#cite_note-107">
        [107]
       </a>
      </sup>
      As with photovoltaics,
      <a href="/wiki/Solar_water_heating" title="Solar water heating">
       solar water heating
      </a>
      attracted renewed attention as a result of the oil crises in the 1970s but interest subsided in the 1980s due to falling petroleum prices. Development in the solar water heating sector progressed steadily throughout the 1990s and annual growth rates have averaged 20% since 1999.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SWH_2008_26-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-SWH_2008-26">
        [26]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Although generally underestimated, solar water heating and cooling is by far the most widely deployed solar technology with an estimated capacity of 154 GW as of 2007.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SWH_2008_26-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-SWH_2008-26">
        [26]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">
       International Energy Agency
      </a>
      has said that solar energy can make considerable contributions to solving some of the most urgent problems the world now faces:
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ie11_1-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-ie11-1">
        [1]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <blockquote>
      <p>
       The development of affordable, inexhaustible and clean solar energy technologies will have huge longer-term benefits. It will increase countries’ energy security through reliance on an indigenous, inexhaustible and mostly import-independent resource, enhance sustainability, reduce pollution, lower the costs of mitigating climate change, and keep fossil fuel prices lower than otherwise. These advantages are global. Hence the additional costs of the incentives for early deployment should be considered learning investments; they must be wisely spent and need to be widely shared.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ie11_1-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-ie11-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      In 2011, a report by the
      <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">
       International Energy Agency
      </a>
      found that solar energy technologies such as photovoltaics, solar hot water and concentrated solar power could provide a third of the world’s energy by 2060 if politicians commit to limiting
      <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">
       climate change
      </a>
      . The energy from the sun could play a key role in de-carbonizing the global economy alongside improvements in
      <a href="/wiki/Efficient_energy_use" title="Efficient energy use">
       energy efficiency
      </a>
      and imposing costs on
      <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">
       greenhouse gas
      </a>
      emitters. "The strength of solar is the incredible variety and flexibility of applications, from small scale to big scale".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108">
       <a href="#cite_note-108">
        [108]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <blockquote class="templatequote">
      <p>
       We have proved ... that after our stores of oil and coal are exhausted the human race can receive unlimited power from the rays of the sun.
      </p>
      <div class="templatequotecite">
       <cite>
        —
        <a href="/wiki/Frank_Shuman" title="Frank Shuman">
         Frank Shuman
        </a>
        , New York Times, 2 July 1916
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes.com_23-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-nytimes.com-23">
          [23]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </cite>
      </div>
     </blockquote>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="ISO_standards">
       ISO standards
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/International_Organization_for_Standardization" title="International Organization for Standardization">
       International Organization for Standardization
      </a>
      has established several standards relating to solar energy equipment. For example, ISO 9050 relates to glass in building while ISO 10217 relates to the materials used in solar water heaters.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">
       See also
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright" role="navigation" style="margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em;border:solid #aaa 1px">
      <ul style="display:table;box-sizing:border-box;padding:0.1em;max-width:175px;background:#f9f9f9;font-size:85%;line-height:110%;font-style:italic;font-weight:bold">
       <li style="display:table-row">
        <span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wind-turbine-icon.svg">
          <img alt="icon" class="noviewer" data-file-height="128" data-file-width="128" height="28" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Wind-turbine-icon.svg/28px-Wind-turbine-icon.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Wind-turbine-icon.svg/42px-Wind-turbine-icon.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Wind-turbine-icon.svg/56px-Wind-turbine-icon.svg.png 2x" width="28"/>
         </a>
        </span>
        <span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em 0.2em 0.2em 0.3em;vertical-align:middle">

          Renewable energy portal
         </a>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li style="display:table-row">

         <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">
          doi
         </a>
         :
         <a class="external text" href="//dx.doi.org/10.1016%2Fj.solener.2005.06.007" rel="nofollow">
          10.1016/j.solener.2005.06.007
         </a>
         .
        </cite>
        <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASolar+energy&amp;rft.atitle=Hydrogen+production+via+the+solar+thermal+decarbonization+of+fossil+fuels&amp;rft.au=et.+al.&amp;rft.aufirst=P.V.&amp;rft.aulast=Zedtwitz&amp;rft.date=2006&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=info%3Abibcode%2F2006SoEn...80.1333Z&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1016%2Fj.solener.2005.06.007&amp;rft.issue=10&amp;rft.jtitle=Solar+Energy&amp;rft.pages=1333-7&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=80">
         <span style="display:none;">
         </span>
        </span>
       </li>
